The Qur'anic Basis for the Rule That Legal Obligation Does Not Exceed Capacity
Author gives second example of usuli inference from Qur'an

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the verse 'God does not charge a soul except with what is within its capacity' (2:286) establishes the impermissibility of taklif ma la yutaq (charging with what is beyond capacity).

Core Idea
The negation of impossible obligation is a foundational legal principle grounded in the Qur'an.

The usuliyyun rely on Qur'an 2:286 to establish that Islamic law never imposes obligations beyond human capacity. In this lesson, we analyze their inference from the verse's negation of such charging. The linguistic analysis of 'capacity' (wus') and the divine negation are key to understanding this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the verse and the rule
The verse: 'God does not charge a soul except with what is within its capacity' (2:286). The usuliyyun use it to prove that taklif ma la yutaq is impermissible in Shari'a.
2
Analyze the linguistic term 'capacity' (wus')
The passage notes that 'wus'' in Arabic means ability and effort. This indicates that the verse excludes obligations that require impossible acts.
The linguistic grounding ensures the rule is not arbitrary.
3
Understand the logical inference
God negates (nada) taking a soul to task beyond its capacity. Therefore, any ruling that demands more than a person can bear is invalid. The negation is absolute in the Shari'a.
This is an immediate inference from a divine statement.
4
Apply to usul al-fiqh
This verse proves that all obligations in Islam are within human capacity. It prevents rejection of rulings on grounds of inability, e.g., prayer for the insane is lifted.
The rule is used in furu' al-fiqh to exempt those unable to perform duties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما مألوف العرب في الخطاب، فقد دعا الأصوليين إلى الاهتمام به نزول النص القرآني، وكذا ورود السنة النبوية على وفق هذا المألوف، وطبقا لقواعده ومبادئه، بحيث لا يمكن فهم نصوص الوحي إلا في إطار قواعد اللغة العربية ومألوف العرب في الخطاب. ولذلك فإن الأصوليين لم يكتفوا باستنباط القواعد اللغوية وتقريرها لتفسير النصوص، بل راحوا يستقرئون الأدلة من واقع مألوف العرب في الخطاب لإسناد هذه القواعد وإثباتها . وفيما يلي نماذج من استدلالات (1) الأصوليين بالأدلة النقلية : أ - استدلال الأصوليين بالكتاب على القواعد الأصولية : من ذلك ؛ استدلالهم على اقتضاء الأمر المجرد عن القرينة الإيجاب، بقوله تعالى : هو ... فَلْيَحْذَرِ الَّذِينَ يُخَالِفُونَ عَنْ أَمْرِهِ أَن تُصِيبَهُمْ فِتْنَةٌ أَوْ يُصِيبَهُمْ عَذَابٌ اليو (2) ، فإن الله قد توعد الذين يخالفون أمر الرسول لا بأن تصيبهم فتنة أو صلى الله عليه وسلم يصيبهم عذاب أليم، ولا يتوعد بالفتنة والعذاب إلا على ترك الواجب، فثبت أن امتثال أمره واجب ذلك أيضا ؛ استدلالهم على عدم جواز التكليف بما لا يطاق شرعًا بقوله تعالى : لا يُكَلِّفُ اللهُ نَفْسًا إِلَّا وُسْعَهَا .. ، ووجه الاستدلال أن الله نفى من شرعه تكليف النفس بما لا تستطيع، فإن الوسع في اللغة هو الطاقة والجهد (5) ومن ذلك كذلك ؛ استدلالهم بمجموع آيتين فأكثر في موضع واحد، بحيث لا تستقل إحدى الآيتين بالدلالة على القاعدة حتى تضم إليه الآية أو الآيات الأخرى، وذلك كما في استدلالهم على أنه ليس من شروط الاستثناء كون المستثنى أقل من المستثنى منه بقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ عِبَادِى لَيْسَ لَكَ عَلَيْهِمْ سُلْطَانُ إِلَّا
As for the customary usage of the Arabs in discourse, it prompted the legal theorists to pay attention to the fact that the Qur'anic text was revealed, and likewise the Prophetic Sunna came, in accordance with this customary usage and its rules and principles, such that the texts of revelation cannot be understood except within the framework of the rules of the Arabic language and the customary usage of the Arabs in discourse. Therefore, the legal theorists did not content themselves with deriving and establishing linguistic rules to interpret texts, but rather they inferred evidence from the reality of Arab customary usage to support and prove these rules. Below are examples of the legal theorists' inferences using transmitted evidence: A. The legal theorists' inference from the Book regarding fundamental legal rules: from that is their inference that a command devoid of context indicates obligation, by His saying: '...So let those beware who contradict the Prophet's command, lest a trial befall them or a painful torment.' (24:63) For God has threatened those who contradict the command of the Messenger with a trial or a painful torment, and He does not threaten with trial and torment except for abandoning an obligation, so it is established that obeying His command is obligatory. Also, their inference regarding the impermissibility of legally charging someone with what is beyond their capacity, by His saying: 'God does not charge a soul except with what is within its capacity' (2:286). The aspect of inference is that God negated in His law charging a soul with what it cannot do, for 'capacity' in language is ability and strength. Also from that is their inference from two or more verses in one place, such that one of the two verses does not independently indicate the rule until the other verse(s) are added to it, as in their inference that it is not a condition of exception that the excepted item be less than the excepted-from item, by His saying: 'Indeed, My servants — you have no authority over them, except...' (15:42) etc.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage presents examples of how usuliyyun (legal theorists) use the Qur'an to establish principles of usul al-fiqh. The author first explains that the Qur'an and Sunna were revealed in line with Arab customary discourse, so understanding revelation requires knowledge of Arabic language rules. The usuliyyun not only derive linguistic rules from texts but also infer evidence from actual Arab usage. Then two main examples are given from the Qur'an: (1) The verse 'Let those beware who contradict the Prophet's command...' is used to prove that a command implies obligation. The reasoning: God threatens punishment for disobeying the Prophet's command; such a threat is only for abandoning an obligation, so obeying the command is wajib. (2) The verse 'God does not charge a soul except with what is within its capacity' is used to prove that legal obligation does not exceed capacity. The word 'capacity' (wus') means ability/effort. Additionally, the passage mentions a third example involving two verses together to show that exception does not require the excepted item to be less than the excepted-from item, citing 'Except My servants among them...' (15:42). These examples demonstrate the method of istidlal (inference) from the Qur'an to support usul al-fiqh rules. The passage guards against the misconception that usul al-fiqh rules are merely speculative; they are grounded in scriptural evidence.

Key Terms
التكليف بما لا يطاق
الوُسْع
لا يُكَلِّفُ اللهُ نَفْسًا إِلَّا وُسْعَهَا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which verse do the usuliyyun use to prove that legal obligation does not exceed capacity?
A) 'Let those beware who contradict the Prophet's command...'
B) 'God does not charge a soul except with what is within its capacity'
C) 'Indeed, My servants — you have no authority over them, except...'
D) 'And whoever does good...'

Key Takeaway
The negation of charging beyond capacity in Qur'an 2:286 is the scriptural foundation for the usul principle that all Islamic obligations are within human ability.

Sabr wa Taqsim: The Dialectical Method of Survey and Division
Author defines sabr wa taqsim, its two types, and gives an example from Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of sabr wa taqsim and explain how burhan al-khalf works.

Core Idea
Sabr wa taqsim eliminates false options to reveal the truth by default.

In dialectical reasoning, one powerful tool is to divide a question into all possible positions and then systematically eliminate those that are false. This method, called sabr wa taqsim, is widely used by usuliyyun to establish their doctrines. In this lesson, we will unpack its two forms and see how al-Ghazali connects it to 'burhan al-khalf' (proof by contrary).

Guided Path
1
Understand the two types of sabr wa taqsim
The author explains two types: (1) enumerating the opponent's possible positions and showing all are false, so the truth is the opposite; (2) enumerating possible grounds for a ruling and showing all but one are false, so the truth is that one.
Type 1 is opponent-focused; Type 2 is ruling-focused.
2
Relate to burhan al-khalf
Al-Ghazali calls this method 'burhan al-khalf': instead of directly proving the conclusion, you prove that its opposite is impossible. Once the opposite is eliminated, the conclusion necessarily follows.
This is a form of indirect proof, common in kalam and usul.
3
Differentiate the two types in practice
In type 1, you identify all positions a disputant could take and refute each. In type 2, you identify all possible rulings for a case and eliminate all but one, leaving the correct ruling.
The example from Mu'tazila (given later) illustrates type 2.
4
Apply the method to a simple fiqh question
Consider the ruling on a new financial transaction: Is it permissible or forbidden? Use type 2: list possible rulings (halal, haram, makruh, etc.), then provide evidence to eliminate all but one. The remaining is the truth.
This is exactly how usuliyyun use sabr wa taqsim to determine ahkam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - الاستدلال بالسبر والتقسيم : مما يعتمده الأصوليون من أدلة لإثبات صحة مذاهبهم الأصولية : الاستدلال بالتقسيم، وهو على ضربين: أحدهما: أن يذكر الأقسام التي يمكن أن يعلق عليها الخصم ويبين فساد جميعها، فيثبت أن الحق في خلافها. والثاني : أن يذكر الأقسام التي يمكن تعليق الحكم عليها فيبين فساد جميعها إلا واحدا منها، فيثبت أن الحق في ذلك واحد»(1) ويسمي الإمام أبو حامد الغزالي هذا النوع من الاستدلال العقلي «برهان الخلف، ويقول عنه هو أن لا يتعرض (أي المستدل للمقصود، ولكن يبطل ضده المقابل له ، وإذا بطل أحد الضدين تعين الضد الآخر .. وحاصل ذلك يرجع إلى تقسيم وسبر وإبطال لبعض الأقسام لتعيين ما بقي من الأقسام، وفيه نوع آخر، وهو : حصر لجملة في أقسام وإبطال جميع الأقسام لإبطال الجملة» (2) ذلك أن المعتزلة قالوا في الواجب المخير أن الواجب فيه هو جميع الخصال لا واحدا غير معين حيث بنوا دليلهم على السبر والتقسيم، فقالوا فيما نقله الطوفي عنهم : خصال الواجب المخير، إما أن تستوي في تحصيل مصلحة المكلف أو لا تستوي فإن استوت بأن كانت مصلحته مثلا في التكفير بالعتق، مثل مصلحته في التكفير بالصيام والإطعام لزم أن يكون جميعها واجبًا، لأن اختيار التكفير ببعضها مع تساويها في المصلحة يكون ترجيحا من غير مرجح، وهو محال .. وإن لم يستو الجميع في المصلحة، بل اختص بها بعض الخصال، أو ترجح فيها، مثل : إن اختص العتق بمصلحة التكفير، أو كان أرجح في حصولها من الصيام والإطعام، وجب أن يتعين ذلك البعض، فيكون هو الواجب عينا، لا على التخيير» (3)
B - al-istidlal bi-l-sabr wa al-taqsim: Among the proofs that the usuliyyun rely on to establish the correctness of their usuli doctrines is istidlal bi-l-taqsim (reasoning by division), which is of two types: One: to mention the divisions that the opponent could adhere to and show the corruption of all of them, thereby establishing that the truth is the opposite of them. The second: to mention the divisions upon which the ruling can be attached and show the corruption of all of them except one, thereby establishing that the truth is that one. And Imam Abu Hamid al-Ghazali calls this type of rational proof 'burhan al-khalf' (proof by contrary), saying about it that it is to not address the intended (i.e., the reasoner's goal), but rather to invalidate its opposite corresponding to it. And when one of the two opposites is nullified, the other opposite is determined. The upshot of that reverts to division, survey, and invalidation of some divisions to specify what remains of the divisions. And it includes another type: enumeration of a whole into divisions and invalidation of all divisions to invalidate the whole. That is because the Mu'tazila said concerning the wajib mukhayyar (optional obligation) that the obligatory in it is all the characteristics, not a single unspecified one, basing their proof on sabr wa taqsim. They said, as al-Tufi reported from them: The characteristics of the wajib mukhayyar either are equal in securing the benefit of the obligated person or are not equal. If they are equal—for example, his benefit in expiation by freeing a slave is like his benefit in expiation by fasting and feeding—then necessarily all are obligatory, because choosing expiation by some of them while they are equal in benefit is preference without a preponderator, which is impossible. And if they are not all equal in benefit, but rather some characteristics are specific or preponderant, such as if freeing a slave is specific to the benefit of expiation or is more preponderant in its attainment than fasting and feeding, then that part must be determined, so it becomes the specific obligation, not an optional one.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage defines the method of al-sabr wa al-taqsim as used by usuliyyun (legal theorists). The author begins by stating that it is a proof method employed by usuliyyun to establish the correctness of their principles. He then divides it into two types: (1) enumerating the possible positions that an opponent might take and showing the corruption of all of them, thereby proving that the truth lies elsewhere; (2) enumerating the possible grounds for a ruling and showing the corruption of all except one, thereby proving that the truth is that one. The author then cites al-Ghazali, who calls this type of rational proof 'burhan al-khalf' (proof by contrary), explaining that it works by not directly addressing the intended conclusion but rather by invalidating its opposite, so that when one of two opposites is nullified, the other must be true. The passage then provides an example from the Mu'tazila concerning the 'wajib mukhayyar' (optional obligation) in the matter of kaffarah (expiation). They argued that the characteristics of the optional obligation (i.e., the options for expiation: freeing a slave, fasting, or feeding) must either be equal in securing the benefit of the obligated person or not. If they are equal, then all are obligatory because choosing one without a preference would be arbitrary (tarjih bila murajjih), which is impossible. If they are not equal, then the one with the greater benefit becomes specifically obligatory. Thus they used sabr wa taqsim to argue that in either case, the obligation is determinate, not optional.

Key Terms
السبر والتقسيم
برهان الخلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the method of sabr wa taqsim, if you enumerate the possible grounds for a ruling and show all but one are invalid, what is established?
A) The truth is the opposite of the remaining one.
B) The truth is the remaining one.
C) All grounds are valid.
D) None are valid.

Key Takeaway
Sabr wa taqsim is a dialetical method that systematically eliminates false positions to establish the truth, and it underlies many proofs in usul al-fiqh and kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a dispute about whether a new type of transaction (e.g., cryptocurrency) is permissible. Use type 2 sabr wa taqsim: list the possible rulings (halal, haram, makruh, etc.) and for each, provide a reason to eliminate it until only one remains. Write a short paragraph showing your reasoning.
?
In a kalam debate, a mutakallim claims that the world must have a creator because it could not have come from nothing. How could an opponent use type 1 sabr wa taqsim to challenge this? Enumerate possible origins (self-creation, eternal existence, etc.) and argue they are impossible, leaving creation as the only option.

Mu'tazila's Use of Sabr wa Taqsim on Wajib Mukhayyar
Author defines sabr wa taqsim, its two types, and gives an example from Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the Mu'tazila argument on wajib mukhayyar using sabr wa taqsim and evaluate its logical structure.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila used sabr wa taqsim to prove that the so-called optional obligation is actually determinate.

The Mu'tazila applied the method of sabr wa taqsim to a classical issue: is the obligation in a 'wajib mukhayyar' (e.g., choosing among expiation options) genuinely optional or is one option specifically required? Their argument, reported here, is a textbook example of the second type of sabr wa taqsim. Let's follow their reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue under dispute
The issue is the nature of the 'wajib mukhayyar' (optional obligation) in expiation (kaffarah). The options are freeing a slave, fasting, or feeding. The Mu'tazila claim that all options are actually obligatory, not one unspecified.
The opponent (e.g., Ash'aris) might argue that any one fulfills the obligation.
2
State the division (taqsim)
The Mu'tazila divide the case into two possibilities: either all options are equal in securing the obligated benefit, or they are not equal (i.e., one is superior).
This is a complete logical division; there is no third possibility.
3
Eliminate the first possibility
If they are all equal, then choosing one over another without a reason would be arbitrary (tarjih bila murajjih). Since arbitrary choice is impossible, this leads to the conclusion that all are obligatory.
The impossibility of preference without a preponderator is a key principle in kalam.
4
Eliminate the second possibility
If they are not equal, then the one with the greater benefit must be specifically obligatory (wajib 'ayni). Thus, again, the obligation is not genuinely optional.
This covers the remaining alternative.
5
Reach the conclusion
In either case, the result is that the obligation is determinate: either all are obligatory (if equal) or one specific is obligatory (if not equal). Therefore, the so-called 'optional obligation' is actually not optional.
The conclusion follows necessarily from the division and elimination.

Qiyas al-Awla: A Fortiori Analogy in Jadal
Author explains qiyas al-awla with al-Shirazi's example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and evaluate a qiyas al-awla argument using a fortiori reasoning grounded in a stronger reason.

Core Idea
Qiyas al-awla: the disputed case is more deserving of the ruling than the agreed case.

In dialectical debates (jadal), disputants often need to argue that a ruling should apply to a new case even more strongly than to a case already agreed upon. This is qiyas al-awla. This lesson unpacks its structure and shows you how to apply it in Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the agreed-upon case (asl)
Find the case that both disputants accept as valid. In al-Shirazi's example, it is the permissibility of abrogating the Qur'an by the Qur'an.
2
Identify the ruling (hukm) of the agreed case
Determine the ruling that applies to the agreed case. Here, it is 'permissible' (ja'iz).
3
Identify the disputed case (far') and the stronger reason
Find the disputed case and the reason why it is more deserving of the ruling. Al-Shirazi argues that the Qur'an (which is higher in rank) abrogating the Sunnah is even more fitting than the Qur'an abrogating itself.
The stronger reason must be intrinsic to the disputed case, not arbitrary.
4
Formulate the a fortiori argument
State the argument in 'first-figure' form: if the ruling applies to the lesser, then it applies to the greater a fortiori.
The strength of the reason (al-'illa) is the key; it must be common but stronger in the disputed case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ج - الاستدلال بقياس الأولى : والمراد به أن يقيس المستدل المسألة المتنازع فيها على مسألة سابقة لها لا خلاف فيها من جهة أن المختلف فيها أحق بالحكم من المتفق عليها وأولى به منها . من ذلك ما استدل به الشيرازي لإثبات جواز نسخ السنة بالكتاب ; قال : فصل : وهل يجوز نسخ السنة بالكتاب؟ للشافعي فيه قولان: أحدهما : أنه لا يجوز، والثاني: أنه يجوز، وهو الأصح. والدليل عليه : أنه إذا جاز نسخ الكتاب بالكتاب وهما في المنزلة واحد، فلأن يجوز نسخ السنة بالكتاب وهو أعلى منها - أولى» (1) . د - قياس المختلف فيه على المتفق عليه : وذلك أن يقيس المستدل المسألة المتنازع فيها على مسألة متفق عليها بين المتخالفين، لما بينهما من شبه من ذلك ما أورده الطوفي في ذلك ما أورده الطوفي في مسألة الصلاة في الدار المغصوبة : . . . السيد لو قال لعبده خط هذا الثوب ولا تدخل هذه الدار، فخاط العبد الثوب في الدار المذكورة، لعُدَّ مطيعًا لسيده ، عاصيا له، باعتبار الجهتين، أي : مطيعا له من جهة امتثال أمره بخياطة الثوب عاصيا له من جهة ارتكاب نهيه بدخول الدار التي نهاه عن دخولها. وإذا ثبت هذا في هذه الصورة، فالصلاة في الموضع المغصوب مثلها سواء، لأن الله سبحانه وتعالى أمر عبده بالصلاة ونهاه عن الغصب، وقد جمع بينهما كما جمع العبد الخياط بين خياطة الثوب ودخول الدار .. وأيضًا فإن حركة العبد الخياط في الدار التي نهي عن دخولها وحركة المصلي في المكان ليست منهيا عنها لكونها حركات، وإنما نهي عنها لكونها حركات واقعة في دخوله، وهذا أخص من مجرد كونها حركات والنهي عن الأخص لا يستلزم النهي عن الأعم (2)
C - al-istidlal bi-qiyas al-awla: It means that the reasoner analogizes the disputed case to a prior agreed-upon case, in the sense that the disputed case is more deserving of the ruling than the agreed-upon case and more worthy of it. Among that is what al-Shirazi used to prove the permissibility of abrogating the Sunnah by the Book (Qur'an). He said: Section: Is it permissible to abrogate the Sunnah by the Book? There are two sayings of al-Shafi'i: one: it is not permissible; the second: it is permissible, and it is the more correct. The proof for it: that if it is permissible to abrogate the Book by the Book, and they are equal in rank, then it is even more fitting that abrogating the Sunnah by the Book, which is higher than it, be permissible. D - qiyas al-mukhtalaf fihi 'ala al-muttafaq 'alayh: That is that the reasoner analogizes the disputed case to a case agreed upon between the disputants, due to a similarity between them. Among that is what al-Tufi presented regarding the issue of praying in a usurped house: ... If a master said to his slave: 'Sew this garment and do not enter this house,' and the slave sewed the garment in the mentioned house, he would be considered obedient to his master and disobedient to him according to two respects, i.e., obedient in terms of complying with his command to sew the garment, disobedient in terms of committing his prohibition by entering the house that he forbade him from entering. And if this is established in this scenario, then prayer in a usurped place is exactly like it, because Allah the Exalted commanded His slave to pray and prohibited him from usurpation, and he combined both as the slave tailor combined sewing the garment and entering the house. Also, the movement of the slave tailor in the house that he was forbidden to enter and the movement of the worshiper in the place are not prohibited because they are movements; rather, they are prohibited because they are movements occurring in his entering, and this is more specific than merely being movements, and the prohibition of the specific does not entail the prohibition of the general.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage explains two additional analogical reasoning methods used in jadal (dialectics): qiyas al-awla (a fortiori analogy) and qiyas al-mukhtalaf fihi 'ala al-muttafaq 'alayh (analogy of the disputed case to the agreed case). For qiyas al-awla, the author states that the reasoner analogizes the disputed case to a prior agreed-upon case on the grounds that the disputed case is even more deserving of the ruling. The example from al-Shirazi is about the permissibility of abrogating the Sunnah by the Qur'an: since the Qur'an can abrogate the Qur'an (equal in rank), it is even more fitting that the Qur'an can abrogate the Sunnah, which is lower in rank. For the second method, the author says that the reasoner analogizes the disputed case to an agreed-upon case between the disputants due to a similarity between them. The example from al-Tufi concerns praying in a usurped property. He compares it to a master's command to a slave to sew a garment but not to enter a house: if the slave sews the garment inside the house, he is both obedient (in sewing) and disobedient (in entering). Similarly, a person praying in a usurped place is obeying Allah's command to pray but disobeying His prohibition of usurpation. The movement in the forbidden place is not prohibited because it is movement per se, but because it occurs in the context of entering. The prohibition of the specific does not imply prohibition of the general.

Key Terms
قياس الأولى
qiyas al-awla

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
The example of qiyas al-awla from al-Shirazi is about which legal question?
A) Abrogation of Qur'an by Sunnah.
B) Abrogation of Sunnah by Qur'an.
C) Abrogation of consensus by analogy.
D) Abrogation of analogy by text.

Key Takeaway
Qiyas al-awla extends a ruling from an agreed case to a disputed case when the disputed case has a stronger reason for the ruling.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a qiyas al-awla argument for the permissibility of using analogical reasoning (qiyas) itself: since the texts (Qur'an and Sunnah) are agreed to be sources, and qiyas is derived from them, it should be even more acceptable.
?
Evaluate the strength of the 'illa in al-Shirazi's argument: is the higher rank of the Qur'an over the Sunnah a sound 'illa for a fortiori reasoning? Why or why not?

Qiyas al-Mukhtalaf: Analogizing Disputed to Agreed Cases
Author explains qiyas al-mukhtalaf with al-Tufi's example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the asl, far', and illa in a qiyas al-mukhtalaf argument and assess its strength in a jadal context.

Core Idea
Qiyas al-mukhtalaf: a disputed case is analogized to an agreed case based on a shared attribute (shabah).

When disputants agree on a particular case but disagree on a similar one, qiyas al-mukhtalaf allows the reasoner to argue from the agreed case to the disputed one by pointing to a relevant similarity. This lesson uses al-Tufi's example of prayer in a usurped house to illustrate the method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the agreed-upon case (asl) between disputants
In al-Tufi's example, the agreed case is the slave who sews a garment in a house he was forbidden to enter. Both sides agree on the dual judgment: obedience for sewing, disobedience for entering.
2
Extract the ruling (hukm) of the agreed case
The ruling is that the slave is simultaneously obedient and disobedient because the actions are distinct.
3
Find the similarity (shabah) between the agreed and disputed cases
The similarity is that in both cases, a person performs a commanded act (sewing/prayer) while also committing a prohibited act (entering/usurpation). The prohibited act is specific (entering a specific house/usurping a specific place).
The similarity must be relevant to the ruling.
4
Apply the analogy to the disputed case (far')
Al-Tufi argues that prayer in a usurped house is exactly like the slave's situation: the worshiper is obedient in praying but disobedient in usurping. Thus, the prayer is valid (obedient) while the usurpation is sinful.
The argument also refutes the objection that any movement in the forbidden place is prohibited by noting that the prohibition is specific to the act of entering, not movement per se.

Source Text

The Method of Jadal: Exhaustiveness and Anticipation
Author describes the general method of jadal and the two approaches.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key features of the jadal method in usul al-fiqh, including exhaustiveness and the use of al-fanqala.

Core Idea
Jadal in usul is not debate for debate's sake, but systematic anticipation of every possible objection.

In this lesson, we explore the foundational method of dialectic (jadal) as practiced by scholars of usul al-fiqh. The passage introduces the principle of exhaustiveness—covering all disputed issues and all possible objections. You will learn about the technical term 'al-fanqala,' the formulaic exchange that structures rebuttals, and how this method leaves no argument for opponents to rely on.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of jadal
The passage states that jadal is based on the desire for comprehensiveness in studying disputed issues. What is the goal?
2
Recognize the feature of exhaustiveness
How does the author describe the approach? It says they address issues one by one, refuting any opinion opposing their school.
3
Understand the role of 'al-fanqala'
The passage defines al-fanqala as the formula 'If we say...' or 'If they say... we say to them...'. Why is this important?
4
Summarize the two methods briefly
The passage introduces two methods: that of the early scholars (al-mutaqaddimīn) and that of the later scholars (al-muta'akhkhirīn). Note the distinction.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول
استيفاء كافة المسائل الخلافية الأصولية بالطريقة الجدلية تقوم طريقة الجدل عند الأصوليين أول ما تقوم على إرادة الاستقصاء في دراسة مسائل الأصول المختلف فيها دراسة جدلية، ولذلك فإننا بالاطلاع على موسوعات علم أصول الفقه نجد أن المؤلف في باب الجدل يأتي على مسائل الخلاف الأصولية مسألة مسألة، ويسوق الآراء التي صدرت حول كل واحدة منها، وذلك حرصا منه على نقض الآراء المخالفة لمذهبه أو حتى التي تخالف رأيه الخاص إن كان يعد من المجتهدين داخل المذهب (1) .
والمتتبع لمؤلفات الأصوليين يلاحظ حرصهم في الدراسة الجدلية لمسائل أصول الفقه على تقدير اعتراضات المخالفين والرد عليها، وينطلق ذلك من استقراء كافة الفروض الممكنة حول القضية موضوع البحث، ويستفرغ الأصوليون وسعهم في تجاوز هذه الفروض بردها والإجابة عنها، بحيث لا يَدَعُون لخصومهم حجة أو دليلا يستندون عليه وهذه الصيغة تتمثل فيما يسمى بالفنقلة: «فإن قلنا)، أو ( فإن قال . قلنا له)» (2)
قالوا
·
كما أن المتتبع لمؤلفات الأصوليين - المتقدمين منهم والمتأخرين – يلاحظ أنهم جروا في ذلك على طريقتين اثنتين إحداهما هي طريقة المتقدمين، والثانية هي طريقة المتأخرين.

وتقوم على إيراد أدلة أو اعتراضات المخالفين وإتباع كل دليل أو اعتراض بما يرد عليه أويجيب عنه .

الجدل
ويمثل هذه الطريقة قدامى علماء الأصول في المدارس الفقهية المختلفة، مثل الباجي عند المالكية والشيرازي وإمام الحرمين عند الشافعية، وأبو يعلى عند
الحنابلة .
ويمكن أن نورد هنا نموذجًا لهذه الطريقة من كتاب الباجي (إحكام الفصول في أحكام الأصول)، حيث بعد أن أورد أدلته على أن لفظ الأمر المجرد يدل على الوجوب ولا يصرف إلى الندب إلا بقرينة أورد بعد ذلك أدلة المخالفين متبعا كل دليل بالجواب عنه والاعتراض عليه قال :
«أما
،
هم فاحتج من نصر قولهم في ذلك بأن لفظ الأمر لو كان يدل على الوجوب بمفرده لوجب إذا صُرف إلى الندب بقرينة أن يكون ذلك مجازا لا
حقيقة .
والذي
الجواب أن هذا ليس بصحيح، لأن اللفظ إنما يستغني عن قرينة فيما شهر بالاستعمال فيه ويفتقر إلى قرينة فيما عُرِفَه أن يُستعمل في غيره أكثر، كالغائط هو حقيقة في المطمئن من الأرض ومجاز في قضاء الحاجة، ثم مع ذلك يفتقر إلى قرينة في استعماله في حقيقته ولا يفتقر إلى قرينة في استعماله في مجازه، وإنما ذلك بحسب عرف الاستعمال. احتجوا أيضًا بأن لفظ الأمر إذا ورد، حسن فيه الاستفهام، فلو كان اللفظ موضوعًا للوجوب دون الندب لقبح فيه الاستفهام ألا ترى أنه لو قال : رأيت إنسانًا لقبح أن يسأله هل رأيت هذه البهيمة أو رأيت حمارًا؟ ولحسن أن يسأله : هل رأى ذكرًا أو أنثى؟ والجواب : أن الاستفهام حسن في هذا على معنى إزالة اللبس وما عسى يراد باللفظ ، وهذا كما يقول الرجل : وطئت الجارية، فيحسن أن يسأله : هل أراد الوطء الذي هو الجماع، أو الوطء بالرجل ؛ ثم لو أطلق اللفظ لحمل على
الجماع.
أن
وكذلك لو قلت لرجل : إني اليوم صائم لحسن أن يقول لك : صائم عن الطعام والشراب، أو غيرهما؟ لجواز أن يريد ذلك باللفظ ، وإن كان لفظ الصوم إذا أطلق فإنما يفهم منه الإمساك عن الأكل والشرب، وليس كذلك إذا قال:
First topic: Exhaustiveness of all disputed usuli issues through the dialectical method. The method of jadal among the usuliyyin is primarily based on the desire for comprehensiveness in studying disputed issues of usul in a dialectical manner. Therefore, when we examine encyclopedias of usul al-fiqh, we find that the author, in the chapter on jadal, addresses the disputed usuli issues one by one, and presents the opinions that have been issued regarding each one, out of his concern to refute opinions that oppose his school or even that oppose his own personal opinion if he is considered a mujtahid within the school (1). One who follows the writings of the usuliyyin notices their diligence in the dialectical study of issues of usul al-fiqh in anticipating the objections of opponents and responding to them. This proceeds from surveying all possible assumptions about the issue under investigation, and the usuliyyin exhaust their effort in overcoming these assumptions by refuting them and answering them, so that they leave no argument or evidence for their opponents to rely on. This formula is represented by what is called al-fanqala: 'If we say...' or 'If they say... we say to them...' (2). They said: ... Also, one who follows the writings of the usuliyyin – both early and later scholars – observes that they followed two methods: one is the method of the early scholars, and the second is the method of the later scholars. We will address each of them in a section below. First section: The method of the early scholars. It is based on presenting the evidence or objections of the opponents and following each piece of evidence or objection with a refutation or answer to it. (1) Abd al-Majid Turki: his introduction to the edition of Ihkam al-Fusul fi Ahkam al-Usul by al-Baji, p. 95. (2) Al-Firaq al-Kalamiyya al-Islamiyya, by our teacher Dr. Ali Abd al-Fattah al-Maghribi, may Allah have mercy on him, 1st ed., Maktabat Wahba – Cairo, p. 32. [PAGE_226] This method is represented by the early scholars of usul in the various jurisprudential schools, such as al-Baji among the Malikis, al-Shirazi and Imam al-Haramayn among the Shafi'is, and Abu Ya'la among the Hanbalis. We can present here a model of this method from al-Baji's book 'Ihkam al-Fusul fi Ahkam al-Usul'. After he presented his evidence that the bare word 'command' indicates obligation and is not diverted to recommendation except by a contextual indicator, he then presented the evidence of the opponents, following each piece of evidence with an answer and objection to it. He said: 'As for them, those who supported their position in that argued that if the word 'command' indicated obligation by itself, then if it is diverted to recommendation by a contextual indicator, that would necessarily be metaphorical, not literal. The answer: This is not correct, because a word only dispenses with a contextual indicator in what is commonly used for it, and requires a contextual indicator in what it is known to be used for more often, like 'gha'it' which is literal for a depression in the ground and metaphorical for defecation, yet it requires a contextual indicator for its literal use and does not require a contextual indicator for its metaphorical use; that depends on the custom of usage. They also argued that if the word 'command' is uttered, it is appropriate to ask a question about it; if the word were placed for obligation without recommendation, then asking a question about it would be inappropriate. Do you not see that if one said 'I saw a human', it would be inappropriate to ask 'Did you see this animal or a donkey?' but it would be appropriate to ask 'Did you see a male or a female?' The answer: Questioning is appropriate in this case in the sense of removing ambiguity and what might be intended by the word. This is like when a man says 'I had intercourse with the slave girl', it is appropriate to ask him: Did you intend intercourse which is copulation, or treading with the foot? Then if the word is used absolutely, it is taken to mean copulation. Similarly, if you said to a man 'Today I am fasting', it is appropriate for him to say to you: Fasting from food and drink or from other things? Because it is possible that he intends that by the word, even though the word 'fasting' when used absolutely is only understood to mean abstaining from eating and drinking. And it is not so if he said...'
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage explains the dialectical method (jadal) as employed by scholars of usul al-fiqh. It begins by asserting that the method of jadal among these scholars is fundamentally grounded in the desire for exhaustiveness when studying disputed issues. Rather than selectively addressing points, the dialectician aims to cover all aspects of a dispute. The author notes that in encyclopedic works of usul, chapters on jadal systematically address each disputed issue, presenting the various opinions on it, with the goal of refuting opinions contrary to one's own school or personal ijtihad. The passage then highlights the characteristic feature of this method: anticipating and refuting all possible objections from opponents. This is done by surveying all possible assumptions (furuḍ) about the issue and exhausting effort in refuting them, leaving no argument for the opponent to rely on. The technical term al-fanqala is introduced, referring to the formulaic exchange: 'If we say...' or 'If they say... we say to them...'. The author then states that within the tradition of usul al-fiqh, there are two distinct approaches: the method of the early scholars (al-mutaqaddimīn) and the method of the later scholars (al-muta'akhkhirīn). The first section (al-matalab al-awwal) focuses on the method of the early scholars, which involves presenting the evidence or objections of the opponents and immediately following each with a refutation or answer. The passage then provides a concrete example from al-Bājī's 'Iḥkām al-Fuṣūl fī Aḥkām al-Uṣūl'. Al-Bājī first presents his own evidence that the bare imperative (amr mujarrad) indicates obligation (wujūb) and is not diverted to recommendation (nadb) except by a contextual indicator (qarīna). He then presents two arguments from his opponents and answers them. The first opponent argument: if 'amr' indicated obligation alone, then diverting it to recommendation via a contextual indicator would make it metaphorical. Al-Bājī's answer: this is not correct because a word's need for a contextual indicator depends on customary usage, not whether it is literal or metaphorical; sometimes a literal meaning requires a contextual indicator. The second opponent argument: if 'amr' were solely for obligation, then asking a question about it would be inappropriate. Al-Bājī answers: questioning is appropriate for removing ambiguity, as in other examples. This demonstrates the method of the early scholars: each argument is met with a direct response.

The Early Scholars' Method: Point-by-Point Refutation
Author explains the early scholars' method using an example from al-Baji.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a dialectical exchange from al-Baji and identify the pattern of presenting an objection followed by a direct refutation.

Core Idea
Early usul scholars refute each opponent argument immediately, leaving no gap for doubt.

This lesson delves into the method of the early scholars (al-mutaqaddimīn) as exemplified by al-Baji. You will see how he presents his own argument that the bare imperative indicates obligation, then anticipates and answers two objections from his opponents. This 'point-by-point' approach is contrasted with the method of later scholars who might group objections thematically.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's own thesis
Al-Baji establishes that the bare command (amr) indicates obligation (wujūb) unless there is a contextual indicator (qarīna).
2
Extract the first opponent argument
Opponents argue: if amr indicates obligation alone, then using it for recommendation would be metaphorical. Find this.
3
Analyze al-Baji's response to the first argument
He rejects this by explaining that the need for a contextual indicator depends on customary usage, not literal/metaphorical distinction. How does his example of 'ghā'it' demonstrate this?
4
Extract the second opponent argument
Opponents argue: if amr is solely for obligation, then asking a question about it would be inappropriate. Find this.
5
Analyze al-Baji's response to the second argument
He argues that questioning is for removing ambiguity, and gives examples (human, intercourse, fasting). How does this preserve his claim?

وكذلك لو قلت لرجل : إني اليوم صائم لحسن أن يقول لك : صائم عن الطعام والشراب، أو غيرهما؟ لجواز أن يريد ذلك باللفظ ، وإن كان لفظ الصوم إذا أطلق فإنما يفهم منه الإمساك عن الأكل والشرب، وليس كذلك إذا قال:
First topic: Exhaustiveness of all disputed usuli issues through the dialectical method. The method of jadal among the usuliyyin is primarily based on the desire for comprehensiveness in studying disputed issues of usul in a dialectical manner. Therefore, when we examine encyclopedias of usul al-fiqh, we find that the author, in the chapter on jadal, addresses the disputed usuli issues one by one, and presents the opinions that have been issued regarding each one, out of his concern to refute opinions that oppose his school or even that oppose his own personal opinion if he is considered a mujtahid within the school (1). One who follows the writings of the usuliyyin notices their diligence in the dialectical study of issues of usul al-fiqh in anticipating the objections of opponents and responding to them. This proceeds from surveying all possible assumptions about the issue under investigation, and the usuliyyin exhaust their effort in overcoming these assumptions by refuting them and answering them, so that they leave no argument or evidence for their opponents to rely on. This formula is represented by what is called al-fanqala: 'If we say...' or 'If they say... we say to them...' (2). They said: ... Also, one who follows the writings of the usuliyyin – both early and later scholars – observes that they followed two methods: one is the method of the early scholars, and the second is the method of the later scholars. We will address each of them in a section below. First section: The method of the early scholars. It is based on presenting the evidence or objections of the opponents and following each piece of evidence or objection with a refutation or answer to it. (1) Abd al-Majid Turki: his introduction to the edition of Ihkam al-Fusul fi Ahkam al-Usul by al-Baji, p. 95. (2) Al-Firaq al-Kalamiyya al-Islamiyya, by our teacher Dr. Ali Abd al-Fattah al-Maghribi, may Allah have mercy on him, 1st ed., Maktabat Wahba – Cairo, p. 32. [PAGE_226] This method is represented by the early scholars of usul in the various jurisprudential schools, such as al-Baji among the Malikis, al-Shirazi and Imam al-Haramayn among the Shafi'is, and Abu Ya'la among the Hanbalis. We can present here a model of this method from al-Baji's book 'Ihkam al-Fusul fi Ahkam al-Usul'. After he presented his evidence that the bare word 'command' indicates obligation and is not diverted to recommendation except by a contextual indicator, he then presented the evidence of the opponents, following each piece of evidence with an answer and objection to it. He said: 'As for them, those who supported their position in that argued that if the word 'command' indicated obligation by itself, then if it is diverted to recommendation by a contextual indicator, that would necessarily be metaphorical, not literal. The answer: This is not correct, because a word only dispenses with a contextual indicator in what is commonly used for it, and requires a contextual indicator in what it is known to be used for more often, like 'gha'it' which is literal for a depression in the ground and metaphorical for defecation, yet it requires a contextual indicator for its literal use and does not require a contextual indicator for its metaphorical use; that depends on the custom of usage. They also argued that if the word 'command' is uttered, it is appropriate to ask a question about it; if the word were placed for obligation without recommendation, then asking a question about it would be inappropriate. Do you not see that if one said 'I saw a human', it would be inappropriate to ask 'Did you see this animal or a donkey?' but it would be appropriate to ask 'Did you see a male or a female?' The answer: Questioning is appropriate in this case in the sense of removing ambiguity and what might be intended by the word. This is like when a man says 'I had intercourse with the slave girl', it is appropriate to ask him: Did you intend intercourse which is copulation, or treading with the foot? Then if the word is used absolutely, it is taken to mean copulation. Similarly, if you said to a man 'Today I am fasting', it is appropriate for him to say to you: Fasting from food and drink or from other things? Because it is possible that he intends that by the word, even though the word 'fasting' when used absolutely is only understood to mean abstaining from eating and drinking. And it is not so if he said...'

Commentary
This passage explains the dialectical method (jadal) as employed by scholars of usul al-fiqh. It begins by asserting that the method of jadal among these scholars is fundamentally grounded in the desire for exhaustiveness when studying disputed issues. Rather than selectively addressing points, the dialectician aims to cover all aspects of a dispute. The author notes that in encyclopedic works of usul, chapters on jadal systematically address each disputed issue, presenting the various opinions on it, with the goal of refuting opinions contrary to one's own school or personal ijtihad. The passage then highlights the characteristic feature of this method: anticipating and refuting all possible objections from opponents. This is done by surveying all possible assumptions (furuḍ) about the issue and exhausting effort in refuting them, leaving no argument for the opponent to rely on. The technical term al-fanqala is introduced, referring to the formulaic exchange: 'If we say...' or 'If they say... we say to them...'. The author then states that within the tradition of usul al-fiqh, there are two distinct approaches: the method of the early scholars (al-mutaqaddimīn) and the method of the later scholars (al-muta'akhkhirīn). The first section (al-matalab al-awwal) focuses on the method of the early scholars, which involves presenting the evidence or objections of the opponents and immediately following each with a refutation or answer. The passage then provides a concrete example from al-Bājī's 'Iḥkām al-Fuṣūl fī Aḥkām al-Uṣūl'. Al-Bājī first presents his own evidence that the bare imperative (amr mujarrad) indicates obligation (wujūb) and is not diverted to recommendation (nadb) except by a contextual indicator (qarīna). He then presents two arguments from his opponents and answers them. The first opponent argument: if 'amr' indicated obligation alone, then diverting it to recommendation via a contextual indicator would make it metaphorical. Al-Bājī's answer: this is not correct because a word's need for a contextual indicator depends on customary usage, not whether it is literal or metaphorical; sometimes a literal meaning requires a contextual indicator. The second opponent argument: if 'amr' were solely for obligation, then asking a question about it would be inappropriate. Al-Bājī answers: questioning is appropriate for removing ambiguity, as in other examples. This demonstrates the method of the early scholars: each argument is met with a direct response.

Analyzing a Jadal Exchange on Tard and 'Illah
Explains early jadal method with example objection and response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a jadal exchange by identifying the objection, the response, and the logical distinction between condition and proof.

Core Idea
A condition does not prove what it conditions – absence invalidates, presence does not guarantee.

This passage from Abu Ya'la al-Farra' exemplifies the early method of jadal, where each objection is met with an immediate response. The debate centers on whether the consistency (tard) of a cause ('illah) proves its soundness. You will learn to dissect the dialectical moves and the logical distinction between condition and proof, which is crucial for evaluating arguments in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's first argument
Find the claim that lack of tard invalidates the cause, so its presence must prove soundness. This is an analogical argument: just as absence indicates corruption, presence should indicate soundness.
2
Identify the first response
The response distinguishes between condition (shart) and proof (dalil). It uses the analogy of tahara for prayer: its absence invalidates prayer, but its presence does not guarantee validity. Similarly, tard is a condition, not a proof.
3
Identify the opponent's second argument
The opponent argues that if the cause is consistent, then nothing invalidates it, so it must be sound – there is no middle between sound and invalid. This is a dichotomy argument.
4
Identify the second response
The response counters that the corruption lies in the lack of evidence for soundness. The absence of proof itself is a defect, so the cause is not necessarily sound.
5
Evaluate the method's advantage
Notice that each objection is followed immediately by its answer. This makes the reasoning easy to follow and is a hallmark of the early jadal method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فبعد أن قرر رحمه في باب القياس أن الطرد لا يدل على صحة العلة، لكنه شرط في صحتها، وأورد أدلته على ذلك، أورد أدلة الشافعية القائلين باعتبار الطرد دليلا على صحة العلة، وكان يتبع كل دليل بالجواب عليه، قال: واحتج المخالف بأن عدم الطرد يفسد العلة، فوجب أن يكون وجوده دالا على صحتها ألا ترى أن عدم التأثير عند المخالف لما كان دالا على فسادها كان التاثير دالا على صحتها؟ . والجواب : أن الطرد شرط في صحة العلة وليس بدليل على الصحة، وففد شرط من شروط الحكم يبطله ، ووجوده لا يدل على صحته .. كالطهارة، فقدها يوجب بطلان الصلاة، ووجودها لا يدل على صحتها . واحتج : بأنها إذا اطردت فقد عدم ما يفسدها، فوجب أن لا تكون فاسدة، وإذا لم تكن فاسدة وجب أن تكون صحيحة، لأنه لا قسم بين الصحيح والفاسد، كما أنه إذا لم يكن قديمًا وجب أن يكون محدثا والجواب : أنه قد وجد ما يفسده، وهو عدم الدليل على صحته، وعدم يصححه دليل على فساده (2) . وميزة هذه الطريقة أنها أيسر للفهم ، لورود جواب كل سؤال عقيبه (3)، إذ أن القارئ لا يجد صعوبة في فهم الدليل والرد عليه أو الاعتراض والجواب عليه، لأنهما يتبعان بعضهما مباشرة .
After he (Abu Ya'la) established in the chapter on qiyas that consistency (tard) does not indicate the soundness of the cause, but it is a condition for its soundness, and he presented his evidence for that, he presented the evidence of the Shafi'is who consider consistency as evidence for the soundness of the cause, and he followed each evidence with a response to it. He said: The opponent argued that the lack of consistency invalidates the cause, so its existence must indicate its soundness. Do you not see that the lack of effect, according to the opponent, when it indicated the corruption of the cause, then the effect indicates its soundness? The answer: Consistency is a condition for the soundness of the cause, not evidence for soundness. There is a condition among the conditions of the judgment whose absence invalidates it, but its existence does not indicate its soundness – like purity (tahara): its absence renders prayer invalid, but its existence does not indicate its validity. He also argued: When it is consistent, then what would invalidate it is absent, so it must not be invalid; and if it is not invalid, it must be sound, because there is no intermediate between sound and invalid, just as if something is not eternal it must be originated. The answer: That which invalidates it does exist, namely the lack of evidence for its soundness, and the lack of what would make it sound is evidence for its corruption. The advantage of this method is that it is easier to understand, because the answer to each question follows it, so the reader finds no difficulty in understanding the evidence and the rebuttal or the objection and the answer, since they follow each other directly.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage describes the early method of jadal (dialectics) as practiced by scholars like Abu Ya'la al-Farra' in his book al-'Uddah fi Usul al-Fiqh. The author presents a sample argument regarding the role of 'tard' (consistency) in validating the 'illah (cause) in qiyas. The opponent (the Shafi'is) argues that if lack of consistency invalidates the cause, then its presence should validate it – an analogical argument. The response refutes this by distinguishing between a condition (shart) and a proof (dalil): the absence of a condition invalidates, but its presence does not prove soundness. An example from fiqh is given: purity (tahara) is a condition for prayer; without it prayer is invalid, but having purity does not guarantee the prayer's validity (other conditions must also be met). A second objection argues that consistency eliminates corruption, so the cause must be sound. The answer counters that the corruption is actually present in the form of lack of evidence for soundness. The passage concludes by praising this method for its clarity, as each objection is immediately followed by its answer.

الطرد
العلة
الجواب

B) Because like purity for prayer, its absence invalidates but its presence does not guarantee validity.
C) Because consistency is irrelevant to the cause.
D) Because the opponent's argument is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The early jadal method pairs every objection with an immediate answer; the key logical move is distinguishing a condition from a proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a modern usul al-fiqh debate where a condition is mistaken for a proof. Write a short jadal exchange (objection and response) modeled on this passage.

The Later Method of Jadal: Presenting All Objections First
Defines the later method of jadal and provides a sample argument about new interpretations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the later method of jadal and distinguish it from the earlier method by recognizing the structure of presenting all objections before answers.

Core Idea
In later jadal, all arguments are listed first, then all answers follow — a test of memory and thread.

The later method of jadal, exemplified by Fakhr al-Din al-Razi in al-Mahsul, differs from the early method by presenting all objections in sequence before any responses are given. This requires the reader to hold multiple arguments in mind. In this lesson, you will learn to recognize this structure and understand its purpose in dialectical debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structural description
The passage begins by describing the method: 'presenting all evidences or objections one after another, then following them with answers that rebut them.' This is the hallmark of the later method.
2
Locate the sample issue
Find the question about new interpretations. Notice how al-Razi lists multiple cases: first the case where the new interpretation invalidates the old, then the case where it does not.
3
Recognize the pending objection
At the end, al-Razi starts an objection from Quran 4:115 but does not provide the answer here. In the later method, the answer would follow in a subsequent section.
This illustrates how the later method defers responses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثاني : طريقة المتأخرين وتقوم على إيراد الأدلة أو الاعتراضات كاملة بعضها إثر بعض، ثم إتباعها بما يردها من أجوبة . وخير من يمثل هذه الطريقة من علماء أصول الفقه الإمام فخر الدين الرازي في كتابه «المحصول في علم أصول الفقه». ومن هذا الكتاب نورد نموذجا لهذه الطريقة، يقول رحمه الله في مسألة إذا كان لأهل العصر الأول استدلال أو تأويل، فهل يجوز لمن بعدهم استخراج دليل آخر أو تأويل؟ : إذا استدل أهل العصر بدليل أو ذكروا تأويلا، ثم استدل أهل العصر الثاني بدلیل آخر أو ذكروا تأويلا آخر ، فقد اتفقوا على أنه لا يجوز إبطال التأويل القديم، لأنه لو كان ذلك باطلا وكانوا ذاهلين عن التأويل الجديد – الذي هو الحق - لكانوا مطبقين على الخطأ، وهو غير جائز . وأما التأويل الجديد، فإن لزم من ثبوته القدح في التأويل القديم، لم يصح، كما إذا اتفقوا على تفسير اللفظ المشترك بأحد معنييه، ثم جاء من بعدهم وفسره بمعناه الثاني، لم يجز ذلك، لأنا قد دللنا على أن اللفظ الواحد لا يجوز استعماله لإفادة معنييه جميعا، فصحة هذا التأويل الجديد تقتضي فساد القديم، وأنه غير جائز . أو يقال : إنه تعالى تكلم بتلك اللفظة مرتين وهو باطل، لانعقاد الإجماع على ضده . وأما إذا لم يلزم من صحة التأويل الجديد فساد التأويل القديم، جاز ذلك. والدليل عليه ؛ أن الناس يستخرجون في كل عصر أدلة وتأويلات جديدة، ولم ينكر عليهم أحد، فكان ذلك إجماعا ولا مانع أن يحتج بأمور : أولها : أن الدليل الجديد مغاير لسبيل المؤمنين، فوجب أن يكون محظورًا، لقوله تعالى : ... وَيَتَّبِعْ غَيْرَ سَبِيلِ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ . . . (1) .
The Second Section: The Method of the Later Scholars. It consists of presenting all evidences or objections one after another, then following them with answers that rebut them. The best representative of this method among the scholars of usul al-fiqh is Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi in his book al-Mahsul fi 'Ilm Usul al-Fiqh. From this book we present a sample of this method. He (al-Razi) says concerning the issue: If the people of the first era had an inference or interpretation, is it permissible for those after them to derive another evidence or interpretation? If the people of one era inferred with an evidence or mentioned an interpretation, then the people of the second era inferred with another evidence or mentioned another interpretation, they agreed that it is not permissible to invalidate the old interpretation, because if it were invalid and they were unaware of the new interpretation – which is the truth – they would have been collectively in error, and that is not permissible. As for the new interpretation, if its establishment entails impugning the old interpretation, it is not valid – like if they agreed on interpreting a homonym in one of its meanings, then someone after them interpreted it with its second meaning, that is not permissible, because we have demonstrated that a single word cannot be used to convey both meanings simultaneously. So the validity of this new interpretation would necessitate the invalidity of the old one, and that is not permissible. Or it could be said: Allah spoke that word twice, and that is false due to consensus to the contrary. But if the validity of the new interpretation does not entail the invalidity of the old interpretation, it is permissible. The evidence for this: people in every era derive new evidences and interpretations, and no one has denied them that, so that constitutes consensus. And there is no obstacle to arguing with certain things: The first is that the new evidence diverges from the path of the believers, so it must be prohibited, due to His saying: '...and whoever follows a path other than that of the believers...' (Quran 4:115).
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage defines the later method of jadal, where all arguments are presented first and then all responses are given together, as exemplified by Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's al-Mahsul. The sample issue is whether a later generation can produce a new evidence or interpretation if the earlier generation already had one. Al-Razi presents various cases: If the new interpretation invalidates the old, it is not allowed because the earlier consensus would be wrong. If the new interpretation does not invalidate the old, it is allowed, citing the continuous practice of scholars deriving new proofs. He then anticipates an objection: the new evidence might be considered a divergence from the way of believers (Quran 4:115). This objection is left partially quoted, implying that the later method would handle it in the subsequent response section. The passage illustrates the structural difference from the early method: here, all objections are listed before any answers are given, requiring the reader to follow a longer thread.

الدليل
الإجماع

Key Takeaway
The later method of jadal organizes debate by listing all arguments before any answers, requiring the audience to retain multiple points.

Conditional Reasoning for New Interpretations in Usul al-Fiqh
Defines the later method of jadal and provides a sample argument about new interpretations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Razi's conditional argument on new interpretations and evaluate its validity using the rules of conditional syllogisms.

Core Idea
Al-Razi's ruling on new interpretations hinges on a conditional: if it invalidates the old, it's invalid; if not, it's valid.

In usul al-fiqh, the permissibility of new interpretations (ta'wil) is debated using conditional logic. Al-Razi presents a clear conditional argument: if a new interpretation entails the invalidity of the old interpretation, then it is not permissible. Conversely, if it does not, it is permissible. In this lesson, you will formalize this argument and test its logical validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions
Find the two conditional statements: (a) If the new interpretation invalidates the old, it is not permissible. (b) If it does not invalidate the old, it is permissible.
2
Formalize as conditional syllogisms
Write each as an 'if-then' statement. For (a): If P (new invalidation) then Q (impermissibility). For (b): If not-P then not-Q (or R for permissibility).
3
Examine the evidence
Al-Razi gives evidence for the permissive case: consensus from continuous practice. Check if that evidence supports the conditional.
The evidence is that people have always derived new interpretations without objection, so the antecedent (no invalidation) is affirmed.
4
Evaluate the objection
He raises an objection from Quran 4:115 that might seem to imply the new interpretation is prohibited. Test whether this objection undermines the conditional or addresses a different condition.
The objection likely applies when the new interpretation contradicts the old, which is already covered by the negative conditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Method of Later Scholars in Jadal
Describes the method and illustrates with al-Razi's disputation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structural features of the later scholars' method of disputation and apply it to analyse a sample debate from al-Mahsul.

Core Idea
Later scholars present all arguments in a block, then answer them collectively.

This passage introduces a distinctive method used by later usuli scholars (المتأخرين) to structure debate. Unlike the earlier method of back-and-forth exchange, this approach gathers all proofs or objections first, then follows with comprehensive responses. Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's example from al-Mahsul illustrates how the method works in practice, dealing with the question of whether new proofs or interpretations can be introduced after the early generations.

Guided Path
1
Recognise the two-phase structure
The passage explicitly states the method: present all proofs/objections together, then answer them collectively. Notice how al-Razi first states the problem and then lists three objections before giving three responses.
2
Examine the objections in al-Razi's example
Identify the three objections against new proofs/interpretations: (1) following a different path from believers is forbidden, (2) the Companions as the best nation enjoin all good, so anything not mentioned is evil, (3) if new proof were correct, Companions would have known it.
Each objection is derived from Quranic verses or rational reasoning.
3
Analyse the responses
Al-Razi answers each objection: (1) the verse about following a different path applies only when believers have negated the matter; (2) the verse about forbidding evil implies the new proof is not evil if believers forbade it; (3) Companions may have sufficed with existing proofs and not sought more.
The answers respect the authority of the early generations while allowing room for new ijtihad.
4
Observe the transition to al-Amidi
The passage then notes that al-Amidi followed the same method. His example on consensus based on ijtihad begins with five objections, but the passage cuts off before listing them. This demonstrates the method's consistent application across different issues.
The cut-off is intentional to show the pattern; the full text would continue with the five objections then answers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثاني : طريقة المتأخرين وتقوم على إيراد الأدلة أو الاعتراضات كاملة بعضها إثر بعض، ثم إتباعها بما يردها من أجوبة . وخير من يمثل هذه الطريقة من علماء أصول الفقه الإمام فخر الدين الرازي في كتابه «المحصول في علم أصول الفقه». ومن هذا الكتاب نورد نموذجا لهذه الطريقة، يقول رحمه الله في مسألة إذا كان لأهل العصر الأول استدلال أو تأويل، فهل يجوز لمن بعدهم استخراج دليل آخر أو تأويل؟» : إذا استدل أهل العصر بدليل أو ذكروا تأويلا، ثم استدل أهل العصر الثاني بدلیل آخر أو ذكروا تأويلا ،آخر ، فقد اتفقوا على أنه لا يجوز إبطال التأويل القديم، لأنه لو كان ذلك باطلا وكانوا ذاهلين عن التأويل الجديد – الذي هو الحق - لكانوا مطبقين على الخطأ، وهو غير جائز . وأما التأويل الجديد، فإن لزم من ثبوته القدح في التأويل القديم، لم يصح، ، كما إذا اتفقوا على تفسير اللفظ المشترك بأحد معنييه، ثم جاء من بعدهم وفسره بمعناه الثاني، لم يجز ذلك، لأنا قد دللنا على أن اللفظ الواحد لا يجوز استعماله لإفادة معنييه جميعا، فصحة هذا التأويل الجديد تقتضي فساد القديم، وأنه غير جائز . أو يقال : إنه تعالى تكلم بتلك اللفظة مرتين وهو باطل، لانعقاد الإجماع على ضده . وأما إذا لم يلزم من صحة التأويل الجديد فساد التأويل القديم، جاز ذلك. والدليل عليه ؛ أن الناس يستخرجون في كل عصر أدلة وتأويلات جديدة، ولم ينكر عليهم أحد، فكان ذلك إجماعا ولا مانع أن يحتج بأمور : أولها : أن الدليل الجديد مغاير لسبيل المؤمنين، فوجب أن يكون محظورًا، لقوله تعالى : ... وَيَتَّبِعْ غَيْرَ سَبِيلِ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ . . . (1) . ... وثانيها : أن قوله تعالى : كُنتُمْ خَيْرَ أُمَّةٍ ﴾ (1) خطاب مشافهة، فلا يتناول الا أهل العصر الأول .. ثم قوله تَأْمُرُونَ بِالْمَعْرُوفِ ) من الآية السابقة يقتضي كونهم آمرين بكل معروف فكل ما لم يأمروا به ولم يذكروه وجب أن لا يكون معروفًا، فكان منكرا . وثالثها: أن الدليل الثاني والتأويل الثاني لو كان صحيحا، لما جاز ذهول الصحابة - مع تقدمهم في العلم - عنه . والجواب عن الأول ؛ أن قوله: ﴿وَيَتَّبِعْ غَيْرَ سَبِيلِ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ ، خرج مخرج الذم ، فيختص بمن اتبع ما نفاه المؤمنون، لأن ما لم يتكلم فيه المؤمنون بنفي ولا بإثبات، لا يقال فيه : إنه اتباع لغير سبيل المؤمنين . وأيضًا: فالحكم بفساد ذلك الدليل ما كان سبيلا للمؤمنين، فوجب كونه باطلا وعن الثاني: أن قوله : ... وَتَنْهَوْنَ عَنِ الْمُنكَرِ ... ، يقتضي نهيهم عن كل المنكرات فكل ما لم ينهوا عنه، وجب أن لا يكون منكرا، لكنهم نهوا عن هذا الدليل الجديد فوجب أن لا يكون منكرًا. وعن الثالث : أنه لا استبعاد في أنهم اكتفوا بالدليل الواحد والتأويل الواحد وتركوا طلب الزيادة، والله أعلم (2) . وقد جرى على هذه الطريقة أيضًا الإمام سيف الدين الآمدي، ومن نماذجها عنده ما أورده في مسألة جواز وقوع الإجماع استنادًا إلى الاجتهاد والقياس»، فبعد أن أورد الآراء المختلفة في المسألة اختار الآمدي جوازه ووقوعه، وأنه حجة تمتنع مخالفته، ثم أورد أدلته على رأيه وأتبعها باعتراضات المخالفين عليها مجتمعة، ثم أجاب عليها أيضًا ،مجتمعة ونورد هنا هذه الاعتراضات متبوعة بأجوبتها، قال الله : رحمه فإن قيل : ما ذكرتموه من دليل الجواز معارض بما يدل على عدمه، وبيانه من خمسة أوجه :
The second section: The method of the later scholars. It is based on presenting all proofs or objections completely, one after another, then following them with responses that refute them. The best representative of this method among scholars of usul al-fiqh is Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi in his book 'al-Mahsul fi 'Ilm Usul al-Fiqh'. From this book we present a model of this method. He, may Allah have mercy on him, says regarding the question: 'If the people of the first era had a proof or an interpretation, is it permissible for those after them to derive another proof or interpretation?' 'If the people of an era prove a proof or mention an interpretation, then the people of the second era prove another proof or mention another interpretation, they have agreed that it is not permissible to invalidate the old interpretation, because if it were false and they were unaware of the new interpretation – which is the truth – they would be in agreement upon error, and that is not permissible. As for the new interpretation, if its establishment necessitates impugning the old interpretation, it is not valid. For example, if they agreed upon interpreting a homonymous word with one of its two meanings, then later someone came and interpreted it with its second meaning, that is not permissible, because we have shown that a single word cannot be used to convey both meanings. So the validity of this new interpretation entails the corruption of the old one, and that is not permissible. Or it is said: that Allah, exalted, spoke that word twice, which is false due to the consensus on its opposite. But if the validity of the new interpretation does not necessitate the corruption of the old interpretation, it is permissible. The evidence for that is that people in every era derive new proofs and interpretations, and no one objected to them, so that is consensus. There is no preventing arguing with matters: First: that the new proof is different from the way of the believers, so it must be forbidden, due to His saying: '...and whoever follows a way other than that of the believers...' (Quran 4:115). Second: that His saying: 'You are the best nation' (Quran 3:110) is direct address, so it only includes the people of the first era... then His saying 'you enjoin what is right' from the previous verse implies that they enjoin every good; so whatever they did not enjoin and did not mention must not be good, so it is evil. Third: that the second proof and the second interpretation, if they were correct, it would not be permissible for the Companions – despite their precedence in knowledge – to be heedless of it. The answer to the first: that His saying 'and whoever follows a way other than that of the believers' came in the context of blame, so it is specific to whoever follows what the believers negated, because what the believers did not speak about, neither negating nor affirming, it is not said that it is following a way other than that of the believers. Also: the judgment that that proof is corrupt was not the way of the believers, so it must be invalid. As for the second: that His saying 'and you forbid what is wrong' implies their forbidding every evil; so whatever they did not forbid must not be evil, but they forbade this new proof, so it must not be evil. As for the third: there is no improbability that they sufficed with the one proof and the one interpretation and left off seeking more, and Allah knows best. Also following this method was Imam Sayf al-Din al-Amidi, and among his examples is what he presented on the question of the permissibility of consensus based on ijtihad and analogy.' After presenting the different opinions on the issue, al-Amidi chose its permissibility and occurrence, and that it is a proof whose opposition is impermissible. Then he presented his proofs for his opinion and followed them with the objections of the opponents collectively, then he answered them also collectively. We present here these objections followed by their answers. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'If it is said: What you mentioned as proof of permissibility is countered by what indicates its non-existence, and its explanation is from five aspects:'
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage describes the method of the later scholars (المتأخرين) in jadal (disputation) and provides a concrete example from Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's work 'al-Mahsul'. The key feature of this method is that the scholar presents all arguments or objections in a complete block, then follows them with comprehensive responses. Al-Razi's example concerns whether later generations can produce new proofs or interpretations after the early generations. The discussion presents three reasons why new interpretations might be invalid if they contradict old ones, and then three answers to those objections. Note that the passage also includes the beginning of al-Amidi's treatment of a similar issue, but cuts off at the start of his objections. The method is dialectical: it structures the debate as a series of claims and counterclaims, ensuring that all points are addressed systematically. This approach contrasts with the earlier method (طريقة المتقدمين), which is not detailed here but presumably involved more piecemeal argumentation.

Key Terms
المتأخرين
الجدل
الدليل
التأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key structural feature of the later scholars' method as described in the passage?
A) They present each proof and objection alternately.
B) They present all proofs together, then all responses together.
C) They only use rational proofs, ignoring scriptural evidence.
D) They allow new interpretations only if they contradict the old ones.

Key Takeaway
The later scholars' block method organises disputation into a clear presentation of all objections followed by comprehensive responses, ensuring systematic refutation.
